        <title>The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="A key step in domestication of the grapevine was the transition from separate sexes (dioecy) in wild Vitis vinifera ssp. sylvestris (V. sylvestris) to hermaphroditism in cultivated Vitis vinifera ssp. sativa (V. vinifera). It is known that V. sylvestris has an XY system and V. vinifera a modified Y haplotype (Yh) and that the sex locus is small, but it has not previously been precisely characterized. We generate a high-quality de novo reference genome for V. sylvestris, onto which we map whole-genome re-sequencing data of a cross to locate the sex locus. Assembly of the full X, Y, and Yh haplotypes of V. sylvestris and V. vinifera sex locus and examining their gene content and expression profiles during flower development in wild and cultivated accessions show that truncation and deletion of tapetum and pollen development genes on the X haplotype likely causes male sterility, while the upregulation of a Y allele of a cytokinin regulator (APRT3) may cause female sterility. The downregulation of this cytokinin regulator in the Yh haplotype may be sufficient to trigger reversal to hermaphroditism. Molecular dating of X and Y haplotypes is consistent with the sex locus being as old as the Vitis genus, but the mechanism by which recombination was suppressed remains undetermined. We describe the genomic and evolutionary characterization of the sex locus of cultivated and wild grapevine, providing a coherent model of sex determination in the latter and for transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during domestication."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://genomebiology.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="A key step in domestication of the grapevine was the transition from separate sexes (dioecy) in wild Vitis vinifera ssp. sylvestris (V. sylvestris) to hermaphroditism in cultivated Vitis vinifera ssp. sativa (V. vinifera). It is known that V. sylvestris has an XY system and V. vinifera a modified Y haplotype (Yh) and that the sex locus is small, but it has not previously been precisely characterized. We generate a high-quality de novo reference genome for V. sylvestris, onto which we map whole-genome re-sequencing data of a cross to locate the sex locus. Assembly of the full X, Y, and Yh haplotypes of V. sylvestris and V. vinifera sex locus and examining their gene content and expression profiles during flower development in wild and cultivated accessions show that truncation and deletion of tapetum and pollen development genes on the X haplotype likely causes male sterility, while the upregulation of a Y allele of a cytokinin regulator (APRT3) may cause female sterility. The downregulation of this cytokinin regulator in the Yh haplotype may be sufficient to trigger reversal to hermaphroditism. Molecular dating of X and Y haplotypes is consistent with the sex locus being as old as the Vitis genus, but the mechanism by which recombination was suppressed remains undetermined. We describe the genomic and evolutionary characterization of the sex locus of cultivated and wild grapevine, providing a coherent model of sex determination in the latter and for transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during domestication."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                <p class="c-article-metrics-bar__details"><a href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/metrics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view metrics" data-track-label="link" rel="nofollow">Metrics <span class="u-visually-hidden">details</span></a></p>
            </li>
        </ul>
    </div>

                        
                        
                        
                    </div>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>A key step in domestication of the grapevine was the transition from separate sexes (dioecy) in wild <i>Vitis vinifera ssp. sylvestris</i> (<i>V. sylvestris</i>) to hermaphroditism in cultivated <i>Vitis vinifera ssp. sativa</i> (<i>V. vinifera</i>). It is known that <i>V. sylvestris</i> has an XY system and <i>V. vinifera</i> a modified Y haplotype (Yh) and that the sex locus is small, but it has not previously been precisely characterized.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>We generate a high-quality de novo reference genome for <i>V. sylvestris</i>, onto which we map whole-genome re-sequencing data of a cross to locate the sex locus. Assembly of the full X, Y, and Yh haplotypes of <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> sex locus and examining their gene content and expression profiles during flower development in wild and cultivated accessions show that truncation and deletion of tapetum and pollen development genes on the X haplotype likely causes male sterility, while the upregulation of a Y allele of a cytokinin regulator (<i>APRT3</i>) may cause female sterility. The downregulation of this cytokinin regulator in the Yh haplotype may be sufficient to trigger reversal to hermaphroditism. Molecular dating of X and Y haplotypes is consistent with the sex locus being as old as the <i>Vitis</i> genus, but the mechanism by which recombination was suppressed remains undetermined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>We describe the genomic and evolutionary characterization of the sex locus of cultivated and wild grapevine, providing a coherent model of sex determination in the latter and for transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during domestication.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Dioecy is rare in flowering plants (~ 6%) but over-represented among crops (~ 20%) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Renner SS. The relative and absolute frequencies of angiosperm sexual systems: dioecy, monoecy, gynodioecy, and an updated online database. Am J Bot. 2014;101:1588–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1774e807">1</a>]. In some cases, both wild and cultivated plants are dioecious (e.g., date palm, asparagus, persimmons). Other crops, such as grapevine, papaya, and strawberry, derive from dioecious progenitors and switched to hermaphroditism during domestication. The genes underlying this switch are currently not known in any crop. In <i>Vitis vinifera</i> ssp. <i>sylvestris</i> (<i>V. sylvestris</i>), the wild ancestor of domesticated and cultivated grapevine <i>Vitis vinifera</i> ssp. <i>vinifera</i> (<i>V. vinifera</i>), wild females produce morphologically bisexual flowers, with retracted anthers that produce few and infertile pollen, while male flowers undergo early ovule abortion [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="This P, Lacombe T, Thomas MR. Historical origins and genetic diversity of wine grapes. Trends Genet. 2006;22:511–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1774e829">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e832">3</a>]. Old genetic studies suggested that in grape, sex is determined by one locus with three alleles M, F, and H [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e835">3</a>]. In <i>V. sylvestris</i>, males are MF and females FF, and in <i>V. vinifera</i>, hermaphrodites can be HF or HH [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="This P, Lacombe T, Thomas MR. Historical origins and genetic diversity of wine grapes. Trends Genet. 2006;22:511–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1774e845">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e848">3</a>]. A genetic analysis based on a population segregating for sex, obtained by crossing a <i>V. vinifera</i> genotype and an interspecific rootstock, allowed the identification of a 143-kb sex locus located on chromosome 2 in the <i>V. vinifera</i> reference genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fechter I, Hausmann L, Daum M, Sörensen TR, Viehöver P, Weisshaar B, Töpfer R. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol Gen Genomics. 2012;287:247–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1774e857">4</a>]. However, this particular region is not well assembled in the reference genome (PN40024—version 12X.2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Canaguier A, Grimplet J, Di Gaspero G, Scalabrin S, Duchêne E, Choisne N, Mohellibi N, Guichard C, Rombauts S, Le Clainche I. A new version of the grapevine reference genome assembly (12X. v2) and of its annotation (VCost. v3). Genomics data. 2017;14:56." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1774e861">5</a>], the F and H haplotypes being mixed both on chr2 and on unassembled scaffold_233 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e864">6</a>]. Moreover, other genetic studies in <i>V. vinifera</i> found different size and boundaries for the sex locus (e.g., [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Battilana J, Lorenzi S, Moreira FM, Moreno-Sanz P, Failla O, Emanuelli F, Grando MS. Linkage mapping and molecular diversity at the flower sex locus in wild and cultivated grapevine reveal a prominent SSR haplotype in hermaphrodite plants. Mol Biotechnol. 2013;54:1031–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1774e870">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhou Y, Massonnet M, Sanjak JS, Cantu D, Gaut BS. Evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2017;114:11715–20." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1774e873">8</a>]).</p><p>In <i>V. sylvestris</i>, a target sequencing in a core-collection of males and females using the genes previously identified in <i>V. vinifera</i> confirmed the sex-linkage of some of them, but not all [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e885">6</a>]. It appeared that <i>V. sylvestris</i> has a XY system in which M = Y and F = X. It was also found that the H haplotype of <i>V. vinifera</i> is likely a modified Y haplotype (hereafter called Yh) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e895">6</a>]. Differential expression analysis of male, female, and bisexual flowers in <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> identified two candidate genes for female sterility, in particular <i>APRT3</i>, a putative cytokinin regulator located on chromosome 2 of <i>V. vinifera</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Coito JL, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Silva HG, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:98." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1774e910">9</a>], but no causative mutations have been identified. A genetic and evolutionary model to explain sex determination and switch to hermaphroditism in grapevine is currently lacking.</p><p>To build such a model, we aimed at (i) characterizing first the sex locus in <i>V. sylvestris</i>, the wild progenitor of <i>V. vinifera</i>, and (ii) comparing the sex locus in both wild and cultivated grapevine. We first produced a reference genome of <i>V. sylvestris</i>. We then re-sequenced a <i>V. sylvestris</i> cross and used a segregation analysis to identify sex-linked SNPs and locate the sex locus on the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome. Comparing X, Y, and Yh haplotypes from both <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> obtained by BAC sequencing, we characterized the presence/absence patterns in the sex locus genes. We then compiled the transcriptomic profiles of the sex locus genes during flower development. Combining all this information, we found a few candidate sex-determining genes and identified changes between the Y and Yh haplotypes that could explain the switch from dioecy to hermaphroditism during domestication.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and de novo assembly of a female <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome</h3><p>For this work, we first produced a PacBio-based reference genome for <i>V. sylvestris</i> that was not available yet. We chose a female individual that was sequenced using SMRT-sequencing (120X). Contigs were assembled with falcon-unzip [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chin C-S, Peluso P, Sedlazeck FJ, Nattestad M, Concepcion GT, Clum A, Dunn C, O'Malley R, Figueroa-Balderas R, Morales-Cruz A. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat Methods. 2016;13:1050." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1774e957">10</a>], and the grapevine reference genome (PN40024—version 12X.2, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Canaguier A, Grimplet J, Di Gaspero G, Scalabrin S, Duchêne E, Choisne N, Mohellibi N, Guichard C, Rombauts S, Le Clainche I. A new version of the grapevine reference genome assembly (12X. v2) and of its annotation (VCost. v3). Genomics data. 2017;14:56." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1774e960">5</a>]) was used as a framework to help building pseudomolecules. We obtained a high-quality diploid assembly of 469 Mb with a contig N50 of 1.7 Mb, 98% of the gene content anchored on chromosomes and a BUSCO evaluation of 95% (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Tab1">1</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S1, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM2">2</a>), comparing favorably to other recently published plant genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Raymond O, Gouzy J, Just J, Badouin H, Verdenaud M, Lemainque A, Vergne P, Moja S, Choisne N, Pont C. The Rosa genome provides new insights into the domestication of modern roses. Nat Genet. 2018;50:772–7." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1774e976">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Badouin H, Gouzy J, Grassa CJ, Murat F, Staton SE, Cottret L, Lelandais-Brière C, Owens GL, Carrère S, Mayjonade B. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and asterid evolution. Nature. 2017;546:148–52." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1774e976_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Belser C, Istace B, Denis E, Dubarry M, Baurens F-C, Falentin C, Genete M, Berrabah W, Chèvre A-M, Delourme R. Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps. Nature plants. 2018;4:879–87." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1774e979">13</a>]. We annotated 39,031 protein-coding genes on primary contigs (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Tab1">1</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Location and boundaries of the sex locus</h3><p>On the newly obtained <i>V. sylvestris</i> assembly, the sex locus as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fechter I, Hausmann L, Daum M, Sörensen TR, Viehöver P, Weisshaar B, Töpfer R. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol Gen Genomics. 2012;287:247–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1761">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1764">6</a>] appeared to be fully included in the 5th largest contig of the assembly. To precisely identify the boundaries of the sex locus, a F1 family obtained from a controlled cross of <i>V. sylvestris</i> was generated and re-sequenced. Paired-end Illumina short reads of both parents and 10 offspring yielded 43 to 74 million of paired-end reads per individual (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). To overcome issues due to X-Y divergence, we used an iterative SNP-tolerant mapping procedure [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Prentout D, Razumova O, Rhoné B, Badouin H, Henri H, Feng C, Käfer J, Karlov G, Marais GA. An efficient RNA-seq-based segregation analysis identifies the sex chromosomes of Cannabis sativa. Genome Res. 2020;30:164–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1774">14</a>], eventually mapping 98.2% of reads in average (mapping coverage by individual 14x–28x, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3–6).</p><p>On this data, single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) that segregate with sex in our cross were identified with an empirical approach and with SEX-DETector<sup>++</sup>, a new version of the probabilistic method SEX-DETector that identifies sex-linked genes from patterns of segregation in a cross from RNA-seq data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Muyle A, Kafer J, Zemp N, Mousset S, Picard F, Marais GA. SEX-DETector: a probabilistic approach to study sex chromosomes in non-model organisms. Genome Biol Evol. 2016;8:2530–43." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1785">15</a>], which we further developed here to analyze DNA-seq data. As SNPs close to the sex locus might be genetically linked to the locus in a particular cross only, we used public whole-genome re-sequencing data to validate the sex-linked SNPs detected in this cross (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1</a>a, b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Tables S7–8). We searched for SNPs that were sex-linked in our cross and that were always heterozygous in males and homozygous in females in the validation dataset. We found that the X haplotype of the sex locus spanned 111 kb on the <i>V. sylvestris</i> chromosome 2 (4,810,929 to 4,921,949 bp, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1</a>b, c, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2–5), and we obtained a final dataset of 1865 XY SNPs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM3">3</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="612" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Limit, gene content and synonymous divergence in the sex locus of <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. (a) Detection of sex-linked SNPs on chromosome 2. Left <i>y</i> axis: adjusted SNP number in 10 kb windows, black curve: XY single nucleotide polymorphisms detected by SEX-DETector++ in a cross of <i>V. sylvestris</i>. Purple curve: candidate XY SNPs that show heterozygosity in males and homozygosity in females in a validation dataset of public whole-genome re-sequencing is drawn in purple; right <i>y</i>-axis (blue curve): adjusted mean posterior probability of being XY for SNPs in 10 kb windows. (b) Genotype of nine <i>V. sylvestris</i> individuals (top) and thirteen grapevine cultivars (bottom) in a validation dataset of public whole-genome re-sequencing, at locations of candidates XY SNPs. Red, purple, blue, and white marks represent XX, XY, YY, and missing genotypes, respectively. In the cultivar panel, 11 cultivars are heterozygous (XY) for the sex-linked SNPs, with a Yh haplotype closely derived from Y (see text), while two cultivars (Chardonnay and Riesling) are homozygous YhYh. YhYh genotypes were previously found only in hermaphrodite cultivars [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1836">6</a>]. The black lines highlight the limits of shared XY SNPs between the cross re-sequenced in the present study and the validation dataset. (c) Gene content and annotation and in the sex locus (approximate position). Genes highlighted in red are absent (Δ) or possess a frameshift deletion in the X haplotype (*). Genes highlighted in blue are induced in males. The yellow gene is absent in the Y haplotype. (d) Synonymous divergence between X and Y allele in the sex locus (+/− standard error), reflecting the age of recombination suppression. dS was only computed for genes present in both haplotypes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Full assemblies of the X, Y, and Yh haplotypes</h3><p>To explore the differences in gene content and the structural rearrangements among haplotypes of the sex locus, we sequenced and assembled bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) covering the sex locus in a male of another <i>V. sylvestris</i> population and in <i>V. vinifera</i> cv. Cabernet-Sauvignon. We obtained full assemblies for the X and Yh haplotypes and an assembly with a 13-kb gap in the Y haplotype located in the <i>VviPLATZy</i> gene (see “Methods” section and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Text S1). When aligned, the X haplotype and two BAC contigs covering the Y haplotype showed colinearity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig2">2</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2). When including also the flanking regions in the alignment, no inversion was found (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S6). To identify deletions, we used the alignment between the X and the Y haplotypes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2) as well as the mean mapping coverage in our re-sequencing dataset along the X haplotype, searching for two-fold reductions in males. This revealed eight regions spanning 500 to 5500 bp and affecting one predicted gene (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S3). We detected more insertions of transposable elements in the Y haplotype than the X one (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Tab2">2</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM4">4</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM5">5</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="118" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Structural variation and gene content in the three haplotypes of the sex locus. (A) X haplotype of <i>Vitis sylvestris</i>; (B) Y haplotype of <i>Vitis</i> sylvestris; (C) Yh haplotype of the Cabernet Sauvignon cultivar. The gene positions were derived from a manually curated Eugene annotation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sallet E, Gouzy J, Schiex T. EuGene: an automated integrative gene finder for eukaryotes and prokaryotes. New York: In Gene Prediction Springer; 2019: 97–120." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1912">16</a>]. The transposable elements (TEs) were detected with RepeatMasker 4.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Smit A, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker at. Nucleic Acids Res; 2016. &#xA;http://repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1915">17</a>]; only TEs longer than 300 bp are represented (grayed rectangles). The synteny among sequences was obtained with SimpleSynteny [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Veltri D, Wight MM, Crouch JA. SimpleSynteny: a web-based tool for visualization of microsynteny across multiple species. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44:W41–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1774e1918">18</a>] and orthologous genes connected by curved lines. The graphical representation is not drawn on scale: the distance between the TPP and APRT3 genes is 1.64 and 2.12 longer for the Y and Yh haplotypes respectively, as compared to the X haplotype. Four genes (ETOL1, UAP56, NAP1, Unk) were detected only on the Y and Yh haplotypes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Transposable element density in the X, Y and Yh haplotypes from the reference genome and the BACs</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The comparison between the X, Y, and the Yh haplotypes showed strong similarities between Y and Yh haplotypes and confirmed that the Yh haplotype derives from the Y haplotype (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig2">2</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S3 and S4). We however found that transposable elements’ insertions are more numerous on the Yh haplotype compared to its Y counterpart (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Tab2">2</a>). Most of these insertions are located close to the <i>APRT3</i> gene (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S4). Our assembly of the Yh haplotype was compared with published long-read-based sequences of Yh from Zinfandel [X-Yh] [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Vondras AM, Minio A, Blanco-Ulate B, Figueroa-Balderas R, Penn MA, Zhou Y, Seymour D, Ye Z, Liang D, Espinoza LK. The genomic diversification of grapevine clones. BMC Genomics. 2019;20:972." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2173">19</a>], Cabernet Sauvignon [X-Yh] [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Minio A, Lin J, Gaut BS, Cantu D. How single molecule real-time sequencing and haplotype phasing have enabled reference-grade diploid genome assembly of wine grapes. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:826." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2176">20</a>], and Chardonnay [Yh-Yh′] [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhou Y, Minio A, Massonnet M, Solares E, Lv Y, Beridze T, Cantu D, Gaut BS. The population genetics of structural variants in grapevine domestication. Nature plants. 2019;5:965–79." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2179">21</a>]. Sequence identity was respectively 99.93%, 99.97%, and 99.62%. Dot plots showed a full synteny between the Yh haplotypes. To explore the potential differences among <i>V. vinifera</i> cultivars, we investigated the SNP genotypes at the sex locus in publicly available DNA-seq data of 13 hermaphrodite grapevine cultivars [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhou Y, Massonnet M, Sanjak JS, Cantu D, Gaut BS. Evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2017;114:11715–20." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2185">8</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S9). Out of 13 cultivars, six harbored recombinant genotypes. The 5′ part of the sex locus was always either XYh or YhYh and spanned 93 kb (4.810–4.903 Mb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2192">6</a>]. The remaining part of the sex locus (4.903–4.922 Mb) was either XX or XYh, but never YhYh (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10). The genes essential for the male phenotype should therefore be located in the 93-kb region of the sex locus.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Age of the haplotypes</h3><p>To estimate the age of the sex locus, we calculated the synonymous divergence (dS) between X and Y coding DNA sequences (CDS) of XY gene pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1</a>c) as commonly done [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Charlesworth D, Charlesworth B, Marais G. Steps in the evolution of heteromorphic sex chromosomes. Heredity. 2005;95:118–28." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2212">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bergero R, Charlesworth D. The evolution of restricted recombination in sex chromosomes. Trends Ecol Evol. 2009;24:94–102." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2212_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Muyle A, Shearn R, Marais GA. The evolution of sex chromosomes and dosage compensation in plants. Genome Biol Evol. 2017;9:627–45." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2215">24</a>]. The dS values are all around 0.03 (except two genes for which dS X-Y ≤ 0.01), and the mean and median values for the sex locus are 0.024 and 0.026 respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM6">6</a>). The homogeneity of the dS values suggests that the recombination was suppressed at about the same time for the whole locus. To obtain a rough divergence time from the dS values, we used a molecular clock (7 × 10<sup>−9</sup> base substitution/site/generation time) available for plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ossowski S, Schneeberger K, Lucas-Lledó JI, Warthmann N, Clark RM, Shaw RG, Weigel D, Lynch M. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science. 2010;327:92–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2224">25</a>]. The average generation time in years is difficult to estimate in <i>V. sylvestris</i> as individuals are sexually mature at about 5 years old in the wild but become reproductively efficient later (&gt; 7–10 years old) and old individuals (some more than 100 years old) can be very large and productive [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Levadoux L. Les populations sauvages et cultivées des Vitis vinifera L. Institut national de la recherche agronomique; 1956." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2230">26</a>]. Using a generation time of 5–10 years, we found that the sex locus could be 10.7–21.4 My old, setting a lower bound estimate. To get a higher bound estimate, we computed the average generation time from minimum and maximum age for sexual reproduction (52.5 years) and found that the sex locus could be 112.5 My old. We thus obtain a large range for the age of the sex locus, but it suggests that the sex locus is at least tens of million years old and is not recent.</p><p>We did a similar analysis for dating the Yh haplotype. As the Yh haplotype derives from the Y haplotype, we computed the dS values between Yh and Y alleles of the genes in the sex locus (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM6">6</a>). We found that the mean and the median dS are 0.0065 and 0 respectively, which are much lower values compared to what we obtained for the comparison between the X and Y haplotypes. Using the dS Y-Yh and using the minimum and average generation times mentioned above, we found that the Yh haplotype might be 2.32 to 24.4 MY old. Using a corrected mean (after removal of two outliers with high dS values) dS Y-Yh instead, we found that Yh haplotype might be 0.57 to 6 MY old. Our lower bound estimate is thus much higher than the estimated date of the domestication of grapevine.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Finding candidate male-sterility genes</h3><p>The classical genetic models for sex determination in grapevine suggested that the sex locus contains two genes (with a dominant <i>So</i> = suppressing ovule allele for the first gene, and a recessive <i>sp</i> = suppressing pollen for the second one, see [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2254">3</a>]). According to these models, females are <i>so sp/so sp</i> and males <i>So Sp/so sp</i>; the X haplotype should thus bear the <i>sp</i> recessive allele and the Y haplotype the <i>So</i> dominant one. To identify candidates, we compared the gene content of X, Y, and Yh haplotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig3">3</a>a) and searched for presence/absence patterns and loss-of-function mutations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig3">3</a>b). We also mapped a public RNA-seq dataset of males, females, and hermaphrodites flower buds of <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> at four developmental stages [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ramos MJN, Coito JL, Silva HG, Cunha J, Costa MMR, Rocheta M. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:1095." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2283">27</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11), measuring the total (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>a) and the allele-specific (X, Y, and Yh, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>b) expression of transcripts.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="376" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Presence-absence patterns and frameshifts mutations in X, Y, and Yh haplotypes. <b>a</b> Presence-absence patterns of five genes absent or truncated in the X haplotype of <i>V. sylvestris</i>. <b>b</b> An 8-bp deletion in exon 2 of the <i>INP1</i> gene is shared by X haplotypes and absent in Y or Yh haplotypes. It results in a premature stop codon, leading to a shorter protein. The DOG protein domain, involved in DNA binding, is truncated</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="475" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Total and allele-specific expression of female-sterility candidates during flower bud development. B, D, G, and H represent early development stages of flower development sequenced in RNA-seq in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ramos MJN, Coito JL, Silva HG, Cunha J, Costa MMR, Rocheta M. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:1095." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2335">27</a>]. <b>a</b> Total normalized expression (RPKM) of <i>VviPLATZ</i>, <i>VviFsex</i>, and <i>APRT3</i> in males (blue circles) and females (orange squares) of <i>V. sylvestris</i>, and hermaphrodites (purple triangles) of <i>V. vinifera</i>. Each point represents a biological replicate. <b>b</b>–<b>d</b> Allele-specific normalized expression. Blue dots, orange triangles, and gray diamonds represent Y-specific, X-specific, and summed expression, respectively in the three sexes: hermaphrodite (<b>b</b>), male (<b>c</b>), and female (<b>d</b>). RNA-seq data were genotyped, and the coverage of X and Y variants was extracted from vcf file and averaged by gene. Only variants common to Y and Yh were included in the analysis of hermaphrodite data. Read counts were normalized by library. Each point represents a biological replicate. <b>e</b> Genetic model for the female-sterility mutation in <i>V. sylvestris</i>. <i>APRT3y</i> inhibits the cytokinin pathway, which results in carpel inhibition and ovule abortion. This may be reinforced by the action of <i>VviPLATZy</i> and <i>VviFSEXy</i> by unknown mechanisms. <b>f</b> Modified model in <i>V. vinifera</i>. <i>APRT3y</i> expression is not enough to trigger the carpel inhibition and ovule abortion. Actions of <i>VviPLATZy</i> and <i>VviFSEXy</i> may also be modified</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>First, we investigated the presence of X recessive mutations possibly causing male sterility. We found that the gene at one limit of the sex locus, annotated as <i>INAPERTURATE</i> (<i>INP1)</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhou Y, Minio A, Massonnet M, Solares E, Lv Y, Beridze T, Cantu D, Gaut BS. The population genetics of structural variants in grapevine domestication. Nature plants. 2019;5:965–79." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2421">21</a>], showed a 8-bp deletion in exon2 in all X haplotypes, resulting in a premature stop codon in its DOG domain (involved in DNA binding) and a truncated protein (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig3">3</a>b). We also found that four genes were absent from the X haplotype (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig2">2</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig3">3a</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2). They included a gene previously annotated [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2440">6</a>] as a short homolog of Ethylene-overproducer-like-1 (<i>ETOL1)</i>. Three other genes were also absent from the X haplotype, namely a gene encoding a short peptide of unknown function (Unk), <i>NAP1</i> and <i>UAP56A</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Finding candidate female-sterility genes</h3><p>Second, we searched for dominant mutations causing female sterility in the Y haplotype and for possible mechanisms of reversion to hermaphroditism. The Y-specific genes described above (<i>INP1</i>, <i>ETOL1</i>, <i>UNK</i>, and <i>UAP56</i>) are not expressed at the stage where ovule abortion occurs and are unlikely candidates for female sterility (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S7). Therefore, we searched for differential expression of X and Y alleles in XY gene pairs. Three genes showed higher total expression in males than in females in the sex locus: <i>VviPLATZ</i>, <i>VviFSEX</i>, and <i>APRT3</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>a). <i>VviPLATZ</i> and <i>APRT3</i> showed a similar expression pattern in males, with a twofold induction during bud development. In females, <i>APRT3</i> expression dropped down from early to late flower development. <i>VviPLATZ</i> expression was very low at all stages in females. Remarkably, only the Y alleles of <i>VviPLATZ</i> and <i>APRT3</i> (<i>VviPLATZy</i> and <i>APRT3y</i>) showed a significant induction in male buds (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S8). <i>VviFSEX</i> showed a decreasing versus stable expression in female and males, respectively. Expression of the X and Y alleles were similar in males.</p><p>If these genes are involved in female sterility in males, they should have a modified expression in hermaphrodites. It is the case of all three genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>a). Even though <i>VviPLATZy</i> has a similar expression pattern in both hermaphrodites and males, it is expressed at a lower level all over the floral development in the former than in the latter (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>b). Both <i>VviFSEX</i> alleles show similar expression in hermaphrodites but contrary to males, their expression tend to decline during floral development similarly to females. In sharp contrast with males, <i>APRT3y</i> was not expressed in hermaphrodites, making it a particular interesting candidate female sterility gene. <i>APRT3</i> is the ortholog of a gene encoding an enzyme of cytokinin elimination. We found that <i>APRT3x</i> is expressed both in female and male buds, especially at early stages, and is a ubiquitously expressed gene in grapevine (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S9). We found that all analyzed cultivars possessed at least one <i>APRT3x</i> allele (recombinant <i>APRT3x/APRT3x</i> genotypes were observed but no <i>APRT3y/APRT3y</i>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1</a>b).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">The wild grapevine genome and sex locus</h3><p>Compared to the available <i>V. vinifera</i> reference genome (486 Mb, 2061 scaffolds, 14,634 contigs with a N50 of 102 kb, 26,346 genes and more than 41,000 proteins, see <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/401?genome_assembly_id=214125">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/401?genome_assembly_id=214125</a>), the <i>V. sylvestris</i> diploid reference genome that we generated here has similar size (469 Mb), fewer contigs (591), and a larger N50 (1.7 Mb). Both genomes have a chromosome-level assembly and are of high quality (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><p>In agreement with previous work in <i>V. vinifera</i>, we found that the sex locus is on chromosome 2 of <i>V. sylvestris</i> too. Our study shows that it is 111 kb long, smaller than the lower bound estimates from previous work. Picq et al. found that <i>YABBI</i>, a gene close to the sex locus, was not fully genetically linked to it [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2606">6</a>]. This was confirmed here. The non-recombining region extends from <i>TPP</i> to <i>APRT3</i>; the genes outside of this region are recombining and are not part of the sex locus. They may be part of the sex determination gene network but not as master genes. Note that we use sex locus, sex-determining region, Y-differentiated region, and non-recombining region of the sex chromosomes as synonyms here as in previous work in grapevine. The region that we delineated might however not include sex-determining genes only but also other genes unrelated to sex.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Molecular evolution of the grapevine sex locus</h3><p>We found that the divergence between the X and the Y haplotypes is low but using a molecular clock for plants and assuming a generation time from 5 to 52.5 years, we found that the sex locus may be between 10.7 and 112.5 MY old. These numbers may appear very high given the low dS we found for the sex locus. However, our results are consistent with the <i>Vitis</i> genus being slow-evolving [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jaillon O, Aury JM, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A, Choisne N, Aubourg S, Vitulo N, Jubin C, et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature. 2007;449:463–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2627">28</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cenci A, Combes MC, Lashermes P. Differences in evolution rates among eudicotyledon species observed by analysis of protein divergence. J Hered. 2013;104:459–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2630">29</a>]. The radiation the <i>Vitis</i> genus (including subgenera <i>Vitis</i> and <i>Muscadinia</i>) from a dioecious ancestor took place 28 to 54 MY ago [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wan Y, Schwaninger HR, Baldo AM, Labate JA, Zhong G-Y, Simon CJ. A phylogenetic analysis of the grape genus (Vitis L.) reveals broad reticulation and concurrent diversification during neogene and quaternary climate change. BMC Evol Biol. 2013;13:141." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2643">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ma Z-Y, Wen J, Ickert-Bond SM, Nie Z-L, Chen L-Q, Liu X-Q. Phylogenomics, biogeography, and adaptive radiation of grapes. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2018;129:258–67." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2646">31</a>]. Previous work suggested that the <i>V. sylvestris</i> sex locus might be shared with other <i>Vitis</i> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fechter I, Hausmann L, Daum M, Sörensen TR, Viehöver P, Weisshaar B, Töpfer R. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol Gen Genomics. 2012;287:247–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2655">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2659">6</a>]. A recent study has identified the sex locus in several <i>Vitis</i> species and found it to be conserved in both subgenera [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Massonnet M, Cochetel N, Minio A, Vondras AM, Lin J, Muyle A, Garcia JF, Zhou Y, Delledonne M, Riaz S, et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat Commun. 2020;11:2902." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2665">32</a>]. If we assume that the sex locus is 28–54 MY old, as suggested by these observations, using our molecular clock, we obtain an average generation time of 13.1–25.2 years for <i>V. sylvestris</i>, which is not unrealistic. All these pieces of evidence converge to the idea that the grapevine sex locus is relatively old, at least as old as the <i>Vitis</i> genus itself and perhaps older.</p><p>Previous works have emphasized the importance of inversions (usually on the Y) in suppressing recombination between sex chromosomes (e.g., [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Charlesworth D, Charlesworth B, Marais G. Steps in the evolution of heteromorphic sex chromosomes. Heredity. 2005;95:118–28." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2677">22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Muyle A, Shearn R, Marais GA. The evolution of sex chromosomes and dosage compensation in plants. Genome Biol Evol. 2017;9:627–45." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2680">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang J, Na JK, Yu Q, Gschwend AR, Han J, Zeng F, Aryal R, VanBuren R, Murray JE, Zhang W, et al. Sequencing papaya X and Yh chromosomes reveals molecular basis of incipient sex chromosome evolution. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2012;109:13710–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2683">33</a>]). Here we did not find evidence for inversions between the X, Y, and Yh haplotypes of the grapevine sex locus. Recombination must have been suppressed through another mechanism. Transposable elements are known to be abundant in non-recombining regions, which was also observed here in the Y haplotype of the <i>V. sylvestris</i> sex locus, but TE accumulation is usually seen as a consequence of the absence of recombination, not a cause [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Vyskot B, Hobza R. The genomics of plant sex chromosomes. Plant Sci. 2015;236:126–35." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2689">34</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Tenaillon MI, Hollister JD, Gaut BS. A triptych of the evolution of plant transposable elements. Trends Plant Sci. 2010;15:471–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2693">35</a>]. Unfortunately, recombination modifiers are poorly known in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lawrence EJ, Griffin CH, Henderson IR. Modification of meiotic recombination by natural variation in plants. J Exp Bot. 2017;68:5471–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2696">36</a>]. It will be interesting to explore this in future research.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">A genetic model for the sex determination of wild and cultivated grapevines</h3><p>In the classical genetic models, two genes with a dominant suppressing ovule allele (<i>So</i>) and a recessive suppressing pollen one (<i>sp</i>), respectively, explain sex determination in grapevine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2713">3</a>]. When searching for <i>sp</i>, we identified five candidates: <i>INP1</i>, <i>ETOL1</i>, a short peptide of unknown function, <i>NAP1</i>, and <i>UAP56A</i>. <i>INP1</i> loss-of-function mutant in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> lacks pollen apertures [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Dobritsa AA, Coerper D. The novel plant protein INAPERTURATE POLLEN1 marks distinct cellular domains and controls formation of apertures in the Arabidopsis pollen exine. Plant Cell. 2012;24:4452–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2738">37</a>], similarly to the pollen of female <i>V. sylvestris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="This P, Lacombe T, Thomas MR. Historical origins and genetic diversity of wine grapes. Trends Genet. 2006;22:511–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2745">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2748">3</a>]. We found that <i>INP1</i> expression was highly specific of mature flower buds of <i>V. vinifera</i>, consistent with a role in late pollen development [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Dobritsa AA, Kirkpatrick AB, Reeder SH, Li P, Owen HA. Pollen aperture factor INP1 acts late in aperture formation by excluding specific membrane domains from exine deposition. Plant Physiol. 2018;176:326–39." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2757">38</a>]. Inaperturate sterile pollen evolved independently at least six times in eudicots in association with dioecy [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Furness CA. Why does some pollen lack apertures? A review of inaperturate pollen in eudicots. Bot J Linn Soc. 2007;155:29–48." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2761">39</a>]. It remains to be shown however that the absence of apertures in grapevine female pollen is sufficient to cause sterility in grapevine. Some of the <i>A. thaliana INP1</i> knock-out mutants may be fertile [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Dobritsa AA, Kirkpatrick AB, Reeder SH, Li P, Owen HA. Pollen aperture factor INP1 acts late in aperture formation by excluding specific membrane domains from exine deposition. Plant Physiol. 2018;176:326–39." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2767">38</a>], while those mutants in maize are infertile [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li P, Ben-Menni Schuler S, Reeder SH, Wang R, Suárez Santiago VN, Dobritsa AA. INP1 involvement in pollen aperture formation is evolutionarily conserved and may require species-specific partners. J Exp Bot. 2018;69:983–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2770">40</a>]. <i>ETOL1</i> shows homology to <i>NPG1</i>, which is essential to pollen germination in <i>A. thaliana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Golovkin M, Reddy AS. A calmodulin-binding protein from Arabidopsis has an essential role in pollen germination. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2003;100:10558–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2783">41</a>], and the ethylene pathway has been shown to be determinant in floral morph determination in <i>Cucumis melo</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Boualem A, Troadec C, Kovalski I, Sari MA, Perl-Treves R, Bendahmane A. A conserved ethylene biosynthesis enzyme leads to andromonoecy in two Cucumis species. PloS one. 2009;4(7):e6144." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2789">42</a>]. <i>NAP1</i> encodes a nucleosome assembly protein in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Two homologs of <i>UAP56A</i>, a DEAD-box ATP-dependent RNA helicase, have been shown to regulate programmed cell death during tapetum development in <i>Oryza sativa</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Li X, Gao X, Wei Y, Deng L, Ouyang Y, Chen G, Li X, Zhang Q, Wu C. Rice APOPTOSIS INHIBITOR5 coupled with two DEAD-box adenosine 5′-triphosphate-dependent RNA helicases regulates tapetum degeneration. Plant Cell. 2011;23:1416–34." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2805">43</a>], and disrupting them led to male sterility. None of these three genes were significantly expressed in the early developmental stages of the RNA-seq dataset that were produced to study ovule development (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S7), likely because male sterility occurs later than female sterility in <i>V. sylvestris</i> flowers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ramos MJN, Coito JL, Silva HG, Cunha J, Costa MMR, Rocheta M. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:1095." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2814">27</a>]. <i>INP1</i> and the four X-deleted genes were all present in at least one haplotype in cultivars. These results suggest that several recessive deletion mutations affecting genes involved in tapetum and pollen development may cause the male sterility syndrome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig5">5</a>). We were not able to identify genes that could explain why the non-functional anthers observed in females are reflexed.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02131-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2131_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="428" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Evolutionary genomic scenario of the formation of the X and Y haplotypes and reversal to hermaphroditism. <b>a</b> Reconstructed gene content and order in the hermaphroditic ancestor (before the evolution of dioecy). <b>b</b> Formation of the X and Y haplotype in the dioecious ancestor of all <i>Vitis</i> species. <b>c</b> Formation of the Yh haplotypes through a modification of the expression of the genes involved in female sterility (standard Yh) and/or by a rare recombination event at the <i>APTR3</i> gene (Yh′)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>When searching for <i>So</i>, we identified three candidates: <i>VviPLATZ</i>, <i>VviFSEX</i>, and <i>APRT3</i>, a gene involved in the cytokinin pathway<i>.</i> Exogenous application of cytokinin is sufficient to
