restore female fertility in male <i>V. sylvestris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Negi SS, Olmo HP. Sex conversion in a male Vitis vinifera L. by a kinin. Science. 1966;152:1624." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2879">44</a>]. Previous in situ hybridization work suggested that APRT3 was induced during male bud development and absent in female buds [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Coito JL, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Silva HG, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:98." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2882">9</a>]. We suggest that <i>APRT3x</i> performs essential functions in both <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. In contrast, <i>APRT3y</i> is specifically activated in males, suggesting that this allele form may trigger a decrease in cytokinin concentration in the carpel and cause its abortion (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4c, d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig5">5</a>). Downregulation of <i>VviPLATZy</i>, <i>APRT3y</i>, and perhaps <i>VviFSEX</i> may be sufficient to cause reversal to hermaphroditism (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Text S1). In addition (but not in a mutually exclusive way), transition to hermaphroditism may be caused by recombination upstream of the <i>APRT3</i> promoter leading to the <i>APRT3x/APRT3x</i> genotype that is observed in several cultivars (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig1">1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig5">5</a>).</p><p>We found a great functional coherence in <i>V. sylvestris</i> sex locus with tapetum and pollen genes involved in male-sterility and cytokinin pathway genes involved in female-sterility. Pollen and phytohormone pathway genes have previously been involved in sex determination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Fruchard C, Marais G. The evolution of sex determination in plants. Evol Dev Biol. 2017:1–14. &#xA;https://link.springer.com/referenceworkentry/10.1007%2F978-3-319-33038-9_168-1&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2938">45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Charlesworth D. Young sex chromosomes in plants and animals. New Phytol. 2019;224:1095–107." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2941">46</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Evolution of sex determination in plants: insights from grapevine</h3><p>The dominant theoretical model for the evolution of dioecy and sex chromosomes in plants relies on two sex-determining genes, carrying, one, a dominant female-sterility mutation and, the other, a recessive male-sterility mutation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Charlesworth B, Charlesworth D. A model for the evolution of dioecy and gynodioecy. Amer Nat. 1978;112:975–97." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2952">47</a>]. However, other models are possible [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Fruchard C, Marais G. The evolution of sex determination in plants. Evol Dev Biol. 2017:1–14. &#xA;https://link.springer.com/referenceworkentry/10.1007%2F978-3-319-33038-9_168-1&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2955">45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Charlesworth D. Young sex chromosomes in plants and animals. New Phytol. 2019;224:1095–107." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2958">46</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Renner SS. Pathways for making unisexual flowers and unisexual plants: moving beyond the “two mutations linked on one chromosome” model. Am J Bot. 2016;103:587–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2961">48</a>]. In some species such as wild strawberries, <i>Asparagus</i>, date palms and kiwifruit, two candidate sex-determining genes have been found in agreement with the two-gene model [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tennessen JA, Wei N, Straub SC, Govindarajulu R, Liston A, Ashman T-L. Repeated translocation of a gene cassette drives sex-chromosome turnover in strawberries. PLoS Biol. 2018;16:e2006062." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2968">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Harkess A, Zhou J, Xu C, Bowers JE, Van der Hulst R, Ayyampalayam S, Mercati F, Riccardi P, McKain MR, Kakrana A. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1–10." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2968_1">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Torres MF, Mathew LS, Ahmed I, Al-Azwani IK, Krueger R, Rivera-Nuñez D, Mohamoud YA, Clark AG, Suhre K, Malek JA. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat Commun. 2018;9:1–9." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2968_2">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Akagi T, Pilkington SM, Varkonyi-Gasic E, Henry IM, Sugano SS, Sonoda M, Firl A, McNeilage MA, Douglas MJ, Wang T. Two Y-chromosome-encoded genes determine sex in kiwifruit. Nature Plants. 2019;5:801–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2971">52</a>], while in other species such as persimmons and possibly cucurbits, only one gene seems to be determining both male and female fertility [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Akagi T, Henry IM, Tao R, Comai L. A Y-chromosome–encoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science. 2014;346:646–50." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2974">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Akagi T, Henry IM, Kawai T, Comai L, Tao R. Epigenetic regulation of the sex determination gene MeGI in polyploid persimmon. Plant Cell. 2016;28:2905–15." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2974_1">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang HW, Akagi T, Kawakatsu T, Tao R. Gene networks orchestrated by Me GI: a single-factor mechanism underlying sex determination in persimmon. Plant J. 2019;98:97–111." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2974_2">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Boualem A, Troadec C, Camps C, Lemhemdi A, Morin H, Sari M-A, Fraenkel-Zagouri R, Kovalski I, Dogimont C, Perl-Treves R. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science. 2015;350:688–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2977">56</a>].</p><p>Here we found several candidate sex-determining genes, which seems to rule out the single-gene model in grapevine. However, more than two candidates were identified, which suggest that the evolution of sex determination in grapevine might not have followed exactly the simple two-gene model in which a recessive male-sterile mutation and a dominant female-sterile mutation successively spread in the population evolving towards dioecy. We identified several possible male-sterile mutations, and female-sterility is possibly mediated by several genes of the sex locus. Despite its small size, the sex locus is rather enriched in pollen and ovule genes, it is possible that sex determination initially evolved with two of these genes, and was reinforced later by secondary genes. However, the X and Y haplotypes’ gene contents are conserved among <i>Vitis</i> and <i>Muscadinia</i> subgenera [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fechter I, Hausmann L, Daum M, Sörensen TR, Viehöver P, Weisshaar B, Töpfer R. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol Gen Genomics. 2012;287:247–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2989">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2992">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Massonnet M, Cochetel N, Minio A, Vondras AM, Lin J, Muyle A, Garcia JF, Zhou Y, Delledonne M, Riaz S, et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat Commun. 2020;11:2902." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1774e2995">32</a>]. This suggests that all the sex-determining genes evolved in the dioecious ancestor of the whole <i>Vitis</i> genus (and possibly before).</p><p>Duplication and neofunctionalization have played important roles in the evolution of sex determination in all dioecious plants in which sex-determining genes have been identified [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tennessen JA, Wei N, Straub SC, Govindarajulu R, Liston A, Ashman T-L. Repeated translocation of a gene cassette drives sex-chromosome turnover in strawberries. PLoS Biol. 2018;16:e2006062." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3005">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Harkess A, Zhou J, Xu C, Bowers JE, Van der Hulst R, Ayyampalayam S, Mercati F, Riccardi P, McKain MR, Kakrana A. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1–10." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3005_1">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Torres MF, Mathew LS, Ahmed I, Al-Azwani IK, Krueger R, Rivera-Nuñez D, Mohamoud YA, Clark AG, Suhre K, Malek JA. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat Commun. 2018;9:1–9." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3005_2">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Akagi T, Pilkington SM, Varkonyi-Gasic E, Henry IM, Sugano SS, Sonoda M, Firl A, McNeilage MA, Douglas MJ, Wang T. Two Y-chromosome-encoded genes determine sex in kiwifruit. Nature Plants. 2019;5:801–9." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3005_3">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Akagi T, Henry IM, Tao R, Comai L. A Y-chromosome–encoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science. 2014;346:646–50." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3008">53</a>]. In grapevine, we found that male sterility may have evolved through full or partial deletions as in other systems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Tennessen JA, Wei N, Straub SC, Govindarajulu R, Liston A, Ashman T-L. Repeated translocation of a gene cassette drives sex-chromosome turnover in strawberries. PLoS Biol. 2018;16:e2006062." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3011">49</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Torres MF, Mathew LS, Ahmed I, Al-Azwani IK, Krueger R, Rivera-Nuñez D, Mohamoud YA, Clark AG, Suhre K, Malek JA. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat Commun. 2018;9:1–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3014">51</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Akagi T, Pilkington SM, Varkonyi-Gasic E, Henry IM, Sugano SS, Sonoda M, Firl A, McNeilage MA, Douglas MJ, Wang T. Two Y-chromosome-encoded genes determine sex in kiwifruit. Nature Plants. 2019;5:801–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3017">52</a>]. However, the evolution of the female sterility may have implied the expression divergence of the Y allele compared to the X allele of several genes (the X alleles conserving the ancestral function), a mechanism of neofunctionalization of the Y-allele, not documented before in dioecious plants.</p><p>How the expression of <i>Vivi</i><i>PLATZy</i>, <i>VviFSEXy</i>, and <i>APRT3y</i> was modified remains to be elucidated. Their cis-regulatory regions may have gained some new transcription factor binding sites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Massonnet M, Cochetel N, Minio A, Vondras AM, Lin J, Muyle A, Garcia JF, Zhou Y, Delledonne M, Riaz S, et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat Commun. 2020;11:2902." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3034">32</a>]. Transposable elements may also have played a role. We indeed found accumulation of TEs close to <i>Vivi</i><i>PLATZy</i>, which may have influenced its expression patterns (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig5">5</a>). Remarkably, the most important accumulation of TEs in the Yh compared to its Y progenitor was upstream of the <i>APRT3</i> gene, whose expression is strongly modified in hermaphrodite. TE insertions are known to lead to silencing of nearby genes, as in the case of the genes controlling sexual forms in monoecious cucurbits [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Martin A, Troadec C, Boualem A, Rajab M, Fernandez R, Morin H, Pitrat M, Dogimont C, Bendahmane A. A transposon-induced epigenetic change leads to sex determination in melon. Nature. 2009;461:1135–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3052">57</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Changes in the sex locus and grapevine domestication</h3><p>In hermaphrodites, <i>Vivi</i><i>PLATZ</i>, <i>VviFSEX</i>, and <i>APRT3</i> expression is modified. The modification of <i>APRT3</i> expression was the most drastic (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig4">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig5">5</a>), and it is possible that it is sufficient to restore female sterility. It is interesting to note that the main structural variation between Y and Yh also lies at <i>APRT3</i>. In one Yh haplotype, the <i>APRT3</i> upstream region includes many TE insertions (as discussed above). Another Yh haplotype (Yh′) found on Chardonnay [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhou Y, Minio A, Massonnet M, Solares E, Lv Y, Beridze T, Cantu D, Gaut BS. The population genetics of structural variants in grapevine domestication. Nature plants. 2019;5:965–79." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3091">21</a>] was formed by the recombination between a Y and a X at <i>APRT3</i>, which basically formed a Y haplotype with a <i>APRT3x</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#Fig5">5</a>). The Yh haplotypes are thus only slightly modified compared to the Y one. This may suggest that the switch to hermaphroditism during domestication was easy. This is in agreement with recent studies suggesting that dioecy is rare in flowering plants because it has a high reversion rate to hermaphroditism [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Käfer J, Marais GA, Pannell JR. On the rarity of dioecy in flowering plants. Mol Ecol. 2017;26:1225–41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3104">58</a>]. Hermaphrodites often occur naturally in dioecious plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Ehlers BK, Bataillon T. ‘Inconstant males’ and the maintenance of labile sex expression in subdioecious plants. New Phytol. 2007;174:194–211." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3107">59</a>]. Hermaphroditic <i>V. sylvestris</i> have already been observed in the wild [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Negi S, Olmo H. Studies on sex conversion in male Vitis vinifera L.(sylvestris). Vitis. 1970;9:89–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3113">60</a>] [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coito JL, Silva HG, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, Eiras-Dias J, Amâncio S, Costa MM, Rocheta M. Vitis flower types: from the wild to crop plants. PeerJ. 2019;7:e7879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3116">3</a>]. The Yh haplotypes may have pre-existed domestication as a standing natural variation. Our estimates of the age of the Yh haplotype (based on dS values) indeed support this idea. It is possible that the domestication process started with hermaphroditic individuals. It is also possible that the Yh haplotype was introgressed in the <i>V. vinifera</i> (possibly several times independently) from <i>V. sylvestris</i> or other <i>Vitis</i> species late during the domestication process, a scenario that is consistent with the complex phylogenetic relationship between the X, Y, and Yh haplotypes from different cultivars and wild populations of <i>V. sylvestris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3132">6</a>].</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><p>Our work provides a comprehensive characterization of the sex locus in <i>V. sylvestris</i>, a model for the sex determination in <i>V. sylvestris</i> and the switch to hermaphroditism in <i>V. vinifera</i> and a new reference genome, which is a valuable genomic resource to study diversity and domestication in grapevine. Future genetic and functional studies will be needed to dissect the role of the candidate genes that we found in different aspects of the male sterility syndrome, female sterility, and reversal to female fertility in hermaphrodites. It will be also interesting to understand better the evolution of the grapevine sex locus: how recombination was suppressed between the X and Y haplotypes. The study of the sex locus in many cultivated and wild grapevine populations may help understand more precisely the switch to hermaphroditism.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Sequencing data</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">Whole-genome sequencing (PacBio—long reads) of Sylvestris C1-2</h4><p>The accession of Sylvestris C1-2 with plant code 8500.Col.C1-2, origin of the geographic location Sainte-Croix-en-Plaine, Haut-Rhin (68), France, was used for extraction of high-molecular-weight genomic DNA. The extraction was performed by the CNRGV (Centre National de Ressources Génomiques Végétales) – INRAE Toulouse. They started with 1G of frozen material (− 80 °C), crushed it with liquid nitrogen, and used the Genomic-tip 100/G kit (Qiagen) for the extraction. The SMRT library preparation and sequencing on PacBio RSII platform (P6-C4 chemistry) was done by the IGM Genomics Center at the University of California, San Diego, following the standard PacBio protocols. A total of 129X coverage was obtained in order to perform de novo genome assembly.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec19">Whole-genome sequencing (Illumina—short reads) of Sylvestris C1-2</h4><p>DNA libraries with two different library size from leaves of Sylvestris C1-2 were prepared in order to perform 2 × 151 paired-end short reads sequencing (Illumina). One library had a library size of 740 bp (860 bp with adapters), and the other library had a library size of 392 bp (512 bp with adapters). The two libraries were sequenced on the same flowcell lane. The libraries preparation and Illumina sequencing was performed by the EPGV (Etude du Polymorphisme des Génomes Végétaux) – INRAE. A total of 338,109,086 reads was obtained (~ 100X coverage) for all samples together, used to polish the Sylvestris C1-2 PacBio assembly.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec20">RNA-sequencing of Sylvestris C1-2</h4><p>RNA-sequencing was performed on six samples of the Sylvestris C1-2 accession. Three biological replicates of whole green berries and three biological replicates of whole mid-ripening berries were sequenced. The RNA-seq library preparation was performed with the TruSeq Stranded mRNA Library Prep Kit (Illumina) and was sequenced in paired-end (2 × 100 bp) on HiSeq4000 platform (Illumina technology). Library preparation and sequencing was performed by the GenomEast platform—Strasbourg. A total of 1,113,531,260 reads were obtained for all samples together, used to assemble a Sylvestris transcriptome and perform genes annotation.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec21">Whole-genome re-sequencing of a cross between C1-5 (female <i>Vitis sylvestris</i>) and Martigny_2 (male <i>Vitis sylvestris</i>)</h4><p>These two accessions were crossed in INRAE Colmar ampelographic collection. The accession C1-5 female <i>Vitis sylvestris</i> (plant accession number at INRAE Colmar 8500.Col.C1-5) originated from the geographic location Sainte-Croix-en-Plaine, Haut-Rhin (68), France, and the male accession Martigny_2 (plant accession number at INRAE Colmar 8500.Col.1) was an accession originating from the geographic location Martigny, Swiss. The progeny was grown at INRAE Colmar greenhouses. The DNA samples of both parents, of five male descendants (44613.Col.5026T, 44613.Col.5028T, 44613.Col.5029T, 44613.Col.5033T, and 44613.Col.5053T) and five female descendants (44613.Col.5035T, 44613.Col.5040T, 44613.Col.5046T, 44613.Col.5050T, and 44613.Col.5057 T), were sequenced. The quantity and quality of the DNA extracted from leaves (DNeasy plant mini kit, Qiagen) of 12 individuals from the cross (2 parents and 10 offspring) was checked prior to sequencing. Nanodrop analysis indicated that concentrations were always above 110 ng μl<sup>−1</sup>, and that more than 5 μg DNA was available for all samples. Quality (fragment size) was checked using capillary electrophoresis (Fragment Analyzer) and was satisfactory. Based on quality check, we adapted sonication (using Covaris E220) in order to get mostly fragments of 250 bp. Twelve Illumina libraries were constructed and were pooled for two lanes of sequencing on an Illumina Hiseq 4000 machine in 2 × 100 bp paired-end mode. This yielded between 85 and 149 million reads per individual (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2), which roughly corresponded to 17X to 30X coverage of re-seq data per individual.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Genome assembly, cleaning, polishing, anchoring, and ordering</h3><p>Shortly, the Sylvestris C1-2 de novo genome assembly was performed with Falcon-integrate (Falcon + Falcon-unzip), in order to obtain a diploid assembly (the two haplotypes). With Falcon, PacBio reads were self-corrected, assembly was performed, haplotypes were generated, assembly was polished with Arrow, and finally we obtained a phased diploid assembly of Sylvestris C1-2.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">Genome assembly and phasing</h4><p>The FALCON-integrate 1.8.4 tool used is available on github: <a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON-integrate/tree/1.8.4">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON-integrate/tree/1.8.4</a>. The FALCON parameters used for the <i>Vitis sylvestris</i> genome assembly are taken from the genome assembly of <i>V. vinifera</i> cv Cabernet Sauvignon paper [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chin C-S, Peluso P, Sedlazeck FJ, Nattestad M, Concepcion GT, Clum A, Dunn C, O'Malley R, Figueroa-Balderas R, Morales-Cruz A. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat Methods. 2016;13:1050." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3237">10</a>] (pa_HPCdaligner_option = -v -dal128 -e0.75 -M60 -l2500 -k18 -h1250 -s100 -w8; ovlp_HPCdaligner_option = -v -dal128 -M60 -e.96 -l1500 -s100 -k24 -h1250; pa_DBsplit_option = -a -x500 -s200; ovlp_DBsplit_option = -s200; falcon_sense_option = --output_multi --min_idt 0.70 --min_cov 4 --max_n_read 400 --n_core 8; falcon_sense_skip_contained = False; overlap_filtering_setting = --max_diff 120 --max_cov 120 --min_cov 4 --bestn 10 --n_core 8). The phasing and haplotypes creation was performed with Falcon-unzip, with default parameters. The assembly was polished with FALCON and PacBio reads using Arrow (available in Falcon-integrate).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Assembly polishing and finishing</h4><p>After the FALCON run, we performed additional polishing with Illumina reads. First, Illumina reads were aligned with bwa [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:1754–60." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3248">61</a>] mem and -M option on FALCON’s genome assembly. Then, alignments were filtered with samtools to keep only primary alignments and concordant pairs. Finally, alignments were filtered with bamtools to keep alignments with an edit distance ≤ 5. These filtered aligned reads are used to polish the assembly with the pacbio-util (version 0.2) from pacbio-utilities tool (<a href="https://github.com/douglasgscofield/PacBio-utilities">https://github.com/douglasgscofield/PacBio-utilities</a>). Then, the same alignment was performed on the genome polished with pacbio-utils, and this genome was polished with Illumina reads and PILON (v1.22 - <a href="https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon">https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon</a>). A few haplotigs may have remained in the primary contigs file. A tool is available, purge_haplotigs, to find these false primary contigs in order to move them to the haplotigs file. We used this tool to correct this and to finish our genome assembly (v1.0.4 - commit 6414f68 - <a href="https://bitbucket.org/mroachawri/purge_haplotigs/src/master/">https://bitbucket.org/mroachawri/purge_haplotigs/src/master/</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Quality control of the assembly</h4><p>Assembly statistics such as number of contigs, N50 and L50, were calculated with a home-made script. Genome assembly completeness was assessed with BUSCO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Simão FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3280">62</a>] with the genome mode, the embryophyta_odb9 lineage, and the <i>Arabidopsis</i> species options (version 2.0 - <a href="https://gitlab.com/ezlab/busco">https://gitlab.com/ezlab/busco</a>). Nucmer tool (from MUMmer tool: <a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/mummer/">https://sourceforge.net/projects/mummer/</a> - nucmer (version 3.1) parameters: -maxmatch -l 100 -c 500) and the grapevine reference genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Canaguier A, Grimplet J, Di Gaspero G, Scalabrin S, Duchêne E, Choisne N, Mohellibi N, Guichard C, Rombauts S, Le Clainche I. A new version of the grapevine reference genome assembly (12X. v2) and of its annotation (VCost. v3). Genomics data. 2017;14:56." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3300">5</a>] (PN40024, version 12X.2 - <a href="https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Vitis/Data-Sequences/Genome-sequences">https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Vitis/Data-Sequences/Genome-sequences</a>) were used to align the Sylvestris assembly to the PN40024 reference assembly in order to see completeness of this Sylvestris assembly. Nucmer and grapevine reference genome were also used to anchor and order Sylvestris contigs into chromosomes (with additional parameters –r –q), as it was done in the Chardonnay genome assembly paper [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Roach MJ, Johnson DL, Bohlmann J, van Vuuren HJ, Jones SJ, Pretorius IS, Schmidt SA, Borneman AR. Population sequencing reveals clonal diversity and ancestral inbreeding in the grapevine cultivar Chardonnay. PLoS Genet. 2018;14:e1007807." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3311">63</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">BAC generation and sequencing</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec27">Generation of BAC libraries of a <i>Vitis sylvestris</i> and Cabernet Sauvignon to obtain the sex locus Y, Yh, and X sequences</h4><p>We first chose a <i>V. sylvestris</i> male from a wild population spontaneously growing on a hill forest near Montpellier (France), on the Northern slope of the Pic Saint Loup mountain. Its male phenotype was confirmed over 5 years of observation of the flowers, both on the forest plant and on 5 of its clones planted in the INRAE Vassal Grape Collection in Marseillan, France (introduction name: Lambrusque PSL10; introduction number: 8500Mtp107). High molecular weight DNA was isolated from 40 g of PSL10 young leaves following the cell nuclei extraction method described in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Peterson DG, Tomkins JP, Frisch DA, Wing RA, Paterson AH. Construction of plant bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) libraries: an illustrated guide. J Agric Genomics. 2000;5:1–100." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3334">64</a>] with slight modifications [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Schweiger W, Steiner B, Vautrin S, Nussbaumer T, Siegwart G, Zamini M, Jungreithmeier F, Gratl V, Lemmens M, Mayer K. Suppressed recombination and unique candidate genes in the divergent haplotype encoding Fhb1, a major Fusarium head blight resistance locus in wheat. Theor Appl Genet. 2016;129:1607–23." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3337">65</a>]. The long DNA fragments were partially digested with EcoRI restriction enzyme, and fragments from 100 to 250 kb were selected. Sized and eluted DNA was then ligated into a pAGIBAC-EcoRI cloning vector and cloned into DH10B T1R <i>Escherichia coli</i> strain (Invitrogen). The resulting BAC clones were plated on a solid selective medium and organized in barcoded microplates using a robotic workstation QPix2 XT (Molecular Devices). The BAC library was named Vsy-B-Lamb. It consists of 27,648 clones of 113 kb size in average, representing a 6.4x genome equivalent coverage. The library was replicated for security reason, and the two copies were stored in separate freezers at − 80 °C.</p><p>We also used an existing BAC library of Cabernet Sauvignon (Cabernet Sauvignon clone 412, ENTAV-INRA®), available at CNRGV Toulouse, France, named VVCS-CabernetSauv. Both libraries are today available at CNRGV: <a href="https://cnrgv.toulouse.inrae.fr/fr/Banques/Vigne">https://cnrgv.toulouse.inrae.fr/fr/Banques/Vigne</a>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec28">BAC library screening</h4><p>The bacterial clones were deposited on a macroarray nylon membrane (22 × 22 cm), following a 6 × 6 grid pattern. Three copies of this gridded macroarray were created. To select BAC clones carrying the DNA fragments from the sex locus, we used specifically designed radio-labeled probes. These probes were defined using the sequences published in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3361">6</a>]: VSVV006, VSVV007, VSVV009, VSVV010, VSVV011 (GeneID GSVIVT01001275001, GSVIVT01001277001, GSVIVT01001286001, GSVIVT00007310001, GSVIVT00007312001 respectively). On the Vsy-B-Lamb BAC library, the hybridization of 3 separate pools of probes allowed to spot around 200 putatively positive BAC clones in total; the 30 clones showing the most intense spots were individually tested via real-time PCR using the same sequences used for probe design and 9 clones were validated. The same clustering of the 9 positive clones into two groups of alleles was obtained using two approaches: the first assignation based on their melting temperature curves similarities, and the second based on Sanger sequencing of internal and BAC-end sequence polymorphisms. These two groups correspond to the two alternate alleles expected in a “XY-like” sex region. Internal and BAC-ends sequences were also used to map the BACs on each other, for each allele, with the objective to sequence the minimal number of clones with an optimized overlapping to cover the whole region (minimum tilling paths composed of 3 and 4 clones respectively). The same procedure was used on the VVCS-CabernetSauv library to spot the sex-locus BACs and assign them respectively to the X and Yh allele, Yh designating modified Y haplotypes found in hermaphrodites.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec29">BAC sequencing and assembly of X, Y, and Yh haplotypes</h4><p>The sequences of the 7 sex-region BACs of <i>V. sylvestris</i> and the 6 BACs of Cabernet Sauvignon were obtained via a PacBio RSII sequencer (P6C4 chemistry). Sequencing was done in a pool of 20 individually tagged BAC clones. We used 2 μg of each individual BAC clone DNA to prepare the PacBio SMRT® 10 kb library. GeT-PlaGe Genomic Platform (INRAE-Toulouse, France) handled the sample loading on the RSII device and the data retrieval. We performed the detection and removal of residual <i>E. coli</i> sequences on raw reads. After a second cleaning step consisting in detecting and removing the vector sequences, individually tagged BAC sequences were assembled with the HGAP workflow (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/Bioinformatics-Training/wiki/HGAP">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/Bioinformatics-Training/wiki/HGAP</a>).</p><p>For each of the groups of BACs identified as above (X, Y, and Yh), the BAC sequences were joined using their overlapping, so to form long “haplotigs”. For <i>Vitis sylvestris</i>, one haplotig was then assigned to the “Y haplotype” and the other to the “X haplotype” using the sex-discriminating polymorphisms described by [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3391">6</a>], namely 10 SNPs for VSVV006, 7 SNPs for VSVV007, and 6 SNPs for VSVV009. These SNPs were found to be 100% associated with sex, in a worldwide collection of 22 males, 23 hermaphrodites, and 91 females [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3394">6</a>], and we confirmed that the two haplotigs had either all male SNPs or all female SNPs. We used a similar procedure for calling X and Yh alleles from Cabernet Sauvignon BACs sequencing. Our Cabernet Sauvignon haplotig assemblies were also confirmed by comparison with those publicly available at that time, with a sequence identity of 99.97% over 260 kb, the few differences being all located in mononucleotide repeats ([<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Minio A, Lin J, Gaut BS, Cantu D. How single molecule real-time sequencing and haplotype phasing have enabled reference-grade diploid genome assembly of wine grapes. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:826." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3397">20</a>], see <a href="http://169.237.73.197/CabSauv/">http://169.237.73.197/CabSauv/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Genome and BAC annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec31">Genome annotation</h4><p>RNA Illumina paired-end reads obtained from grape berries of Sylvestris C1-2 (see above) were used to assemble a transcriptome in order to annotate the genome. Prinseq-lite [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Schmieder R, Edwards R. Quality control and preprocessing of metagenomic datasets. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:863–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3420">66</a>], Ribopicker [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Schmieder R, Lim YW, Edwards R. Identification and removal of ribosomal RNA sequences from metatranscriptomes. Bioinformatics. 2012;28:433–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3423">67</a>], and STAR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Dobin A, Davis CA, Schlesinger F, Drenkow J, Zaleski C, Jha S, Batut P, Chaisson M, Gingeras TR. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics. 2013;29:15–21." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3426">68</a>] were used to clean the data, and the assembly was made using Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:644." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3429">69</a>]. We assembled 398,189 putative transcripts (422,652,106 bp), with a N50 of 2217 bp, which roughly corresponds to the average size of Pinot noir transcripts (2056 bp). TransDecoder [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:644." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3432">69</a>] detected 187,734 CDS with an average size of 832 bp (of which 60,985 CDS are larger than 832 bp). A BUSCO analysis detected 1237 genes, of which 1166 (94%) are complete. EuGene-EP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sallet E, Gouzy J, Schiex T. EuGene: an automated integrative gene finder for eukaryotes and prokaryotes. New York: In Gene Prediction Springer; 2019: 97–120." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3436">16</a>] (Eukaryote Pipeline - v1.4 - <a href="http://eugene.toulouse.inra.fr/">http://eugene.toulouse.inra.fr/</a>) was used to perform genes annotation on the Sylvestris assembly. The protein databases used for the genes annotation were the TAIR10, swissprot, and uniprot plants databases. The transcriptomes used for the genes annotation were our Sylvestris transcriptome, 812 manual annotated genes, and transcript sequences of <i>V. vinifera</i> from NCBI - 2017-11-08. The configuration file with all the parameters used is available as supplementary data (egnep. Vvi.for_Sylvestris.cfg). Primary contigs and haplotigs were annotated separately, with the same parameters. Finally, we obtained two gene annotations, one for primary contigs and one for haplotigs, with different files: gene annotation in a gff3 file, gene/mrna/cds/ncrna/protein sequences in separate fasta files, and some statistics per gene.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec32">BAC annotation</h4><p>The BAC sequences’ annotation (<i>V. sylvestris</i> Y haplotype P2, <i>V. sylvestris</i> Y haplotype P1, <i>V. sylvestris</i> X haplotype, <i>V. vinifera</i> Cabernet Sauvignon Yh and X haplotype sequences and the annotation with EuGene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sallet E, Gouzy J, Schiex T. EuGene: an automated integrative gene finder for eukaryotes and prokaryotes. New York: In Gene Prediction Springer; 2019: 97–120." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3469">16</a>] (Eukaryote Pipeline - v1.4 - <a href="http://eugene.toulouse.inra.fr/">http://eugene.toulouse.inra.fr/</a>)) was started with the same parameters and reference files than for the whole-genome annotation. The protein databases used for the genes annotation were the TAIR10, swissprot, and uniprot plants databases. The transcriptomes used for the genes annotation were our Sylvestris transcriptome, 812 manual annotated genes, and transcript sequences of <i>V. vinifera</i> from NCBI - 2017-11-08. The configuration file with all the parameters used is available as supplementary data (egnep. Vvi.for_Sylvestris.cfg). Then, the annotations specific to BACs were extracted and used as final BACs’ annotation. We did not run Eugene on the 5 BAC sequences alone because EuGene learns from data, and we did not want to introduce a bias due to the low number of BAC sequences. Annotation of both full and partial fragments of transposable elements was carried out on the same BAC sequences with RepeatMasker 4.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Smit A, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker at. Nucleic Acids Res; 2016. &#xA;http://repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3483">17</a>]</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Development of SEX-DETector++</h3><p>We developed a new version of the SEX-DETector software that implements a probabilistic method to study SNPs segregation in a family, taking into account genotyping errors. SEX-DETector++ is coded in C++ and uses additional algorithmic optimization to reduce the running time and memory usage by about two orders of magnitude compared to the original code. SEX-DETector++ thus allows convenient usage on large, genome-wide genotyping datasets for which the original code would require prohibitively large running times and memory. The underlying model is the same as in the original code, but new functionalities were added to deal with genomic data as an input of the method (in the vcf format). The code is publicly available at <a href="https://gitlab.in2p3.fr/sex-det-family/sex-detector-plusplus">https://gitlab.in2p3.fr/sex-det-family/sex-detector-plusplus</a>, where technical and installation details can also be found.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Characterization of the sex-linked region in the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec35">SNP-tolerant mapping and SNP discovery from whole-genome re-sequencing data</h4><p>Divergence between X and Y alleles can prevent SNP discovery. To avoid this issue, we carried out an iterative SNP-tolerant mapping similarly to the procedure described in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Prentout D, Razumova O, Rhoné B, Badouin H, Henri H, Feng C, Käfer J, Karlov G, Marais GA. An efficient RNA-seq-based segregation analysis identifies the sex chromosomes of Cannabis sativa. Genome Res. 2020;30:164–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3519">14</a>]. Raw reads of twelve individuals of the same family (2 parents and 5 descendants of each sex) were first mapped against the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome with gsnap [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wu TD, Watanabe CK. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:1859–75." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3525">70</a>] v2018-07-04 (-m 0.1) in standard mode. At each iteration step, SNP calling was performed: variants were called with samtools mpileup v1.3.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Li H, Handsaker B, Wysoker A, Fennell T, Ruan J, Homer N, Marth G, Abecasis G, Durbin R. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:2078–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3528">71</a>] followed with Varscan v2.4.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Koboldt DC, Zhang Q, Larson DE, Shen D, McLellan MD, Lin L, Miller CA, Mardis ER, Ding L, Wilson RK. VarScan 2: somatic mutation and copy number alteration discovery in cancer by exome sequencing. Genome Res. 2012;22:568–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3531">72</a>] mpileup2snp (Min coverage 8, Min reads2 2, Min var. freq 0.2, Min avg. qual 15, <i>P</i> value thresh 0.01). Additional filters were applied: minimum frequency of variants reads between 0.25 and 0.75 for heterozygote genotypes in individual samples, maximum coverage of twice mode of the Gaussian distribution for each sample, only bi-allelic SNPs, minor frequency variant higher than 0.05, and maximum rate of missing data 0.2. A SNP database was built with gsnap utilities to perform a SNP-tolerant mapping at the next iteration. We carried out two iterations of SNP-tolerant mapping until the rate of discovery of new XY SNPs (see below) became low. Finally, a 4th step of SNP-tolerant mapping with more stringent parameters (-m 0.05, primary alignments only) was performed in order to reduce the rate of false-positive SNPs.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec36">Detection and validation of sex-linked single nucleotide polymorphisms</h4><p>In order to detect SNPs linked to the sex-determining region, we run SEX-DETector++. This yielded 4113 sex-linked SNPs (with a posterior probability high than 0.6), 90.5% of them on chromosome 2. As the sex-determining regions is assumed to be small [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fechter I, Hausmann L, Daum M, Sörensen TR, Viehöver P, Weisshaar B, Töpfer R. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol Gen Genomics. 2012;287:247–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3546">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Picq S, Santoni S, Lacombe T, Latreille M, Weber A, Ardisson M, Ivorra S, Maghradze D, Arroyo-Garcia R, Chatelet P. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC plant biology. 2014;14:229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3549">6</a>] and the low number of offspring in our cross gives us access to few recombination events, the sex-linked region in our analysis may appear larger than the actual sex-determining region (SNP close to the sex locus tend to be linked to sex in a particular cross). To determine the boundaries of the sex-determining region, independent public data were analyzed in order to identify shared XY SNPs between different populations. WG-reseq and RNA-seq data from wild and cultivated grapevines were mapped against the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome with gsnap v2018-07-04; SNPs were called with varscan v2.4.3. We searched for candidate XY-SNPs that were heterozygote in all male samples and homozygote in all female samples of the validation dataset. Validated XY-SNPs spanned 111 kb on chromosome 2 (from 4,810,929 to 4,921,949 bp), which indicated the boundaries of the sex-determining region. In order to complement SEX-DETector++ analysis, we also performed an empirical one. We searched for SNPs that overlapped the sex-determining region and were homozygote in female individuals of the cross and heterozygote in male individuals, allowing two missing alleles. This empirical analysis retrieved all 1406 XY SNPs identified by SEX-DETector++ plus 459 additional ones (+ 32.6%). This final dataset of 1865 XY SNPs was used for downstream analyses.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec37">Determination of the synonymous divergence between X and Y alleles in the sex-linked region</h4><p>The dataset of XY SNPs was used to build X and Y allelic pseudosequences in coding regions, using a custom python script to substitute reference positions by X or Y SNPs respectively in the genome in respect with strand, and to extract and concatenate coding DNA sequences for each gene of the sex locus. The yn00 program of the PAML suite was used to estimate synonymous divergence (dS) with standard error estimation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: Phylogenetic Analysis by Maximum Likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24:1586–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3563">73</a>]. The same procedure was used to calculate the dS between the Y <i>V. sylvestris</i> haplotype and the Yh haplotype of Cabernet Sauvignon.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec38">Structural characterization of the sex-linked region in the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome</h4><p>Structural gene annotations in the sex-linked regions were extracted from Eugene (see annotation section). Predicted proteins were mapped against the NCBI nr database with BLASTP, and functional annotations were manually analyzed. The best hit in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> was used to name the genes. Transposable elements were detected with Red [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Girgis HZ. Red: an intelligent, rapid, accurate tool for detecting repeats de-novo on the genomic scale. BMC Bioinformatics. 2015;16:227." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3584">74</a>] and RepeatMasker 4.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Smit A, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker at. Nucleic Acids Res; 2016. &#xA;http://repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3587">17</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec39">Integrative search for sex-determining genes in the sex locus</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec40">Comparison of gene and TE content in X, Y, and Yh haplotypes</h4><p>In addition to the female whole genome of <i>V. sylvestris</i>, we used the assembled BAC sequences of X and Y haplotypes of <i>V. sylvestris</i> and Yh and X haplotypes of Cabernet-Sauvignon. BLASTN (ncbi-blast v2.2.3) were carried out between pairs of haplotypes (X-Y, X-Yh, Y-Yh, from whole-genome and from BACs). Whole-haplotype comparisons were visualized with CIRCOS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, Connors J, Gascoyne R, Horsman D, Jones SJ, Marra MA. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 2009;19:1639–45." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3609">75</a>] to identify presence/absence patterns. Genic sequences were extracted in all haplotypes using Eugene annotations and BLASTN, and aligned with mafft v7 online service [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Katoh K, Rozewicki J, Yamada KD. MAFFT online service: multiple sequence alignment, interactive sequence choice and visualization. Brief Bioinform. 2019;20:1160–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3612">76</a>] to identify structural differences and mutations. The sequences of the putative sex-determining candidates were blasted against assemblies of other cultivated grapevine genomes (Chardonnay, Cabernet-Sauvignon and Zinfandel) to assess their presence or absence.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec41">Total and allele-specific expression of sex-linked genes</h4><p>We retrieved raw reads from 23 public libraries of female, male, and hermaphrodite bud flowers’ samples from <i>V. sylvestris</i> at four developmental stages [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ramos MJN, Coito JL, Silva HG, Cunha J, Costa MMR, Rocheta M. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:1095." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3626">27</a>]. Reads were mapped on the <i>V. sylvestris</i> genome with gsnap v2018-07-04 (-m 0.1), with a mode tolerant to XY SNPs. Read counts were obtained with htseq-count v0.10.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Anders S, Pyl PT, Huber W. HTSeq—a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:166–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3632">77</a>] and normalized by computing the RPKM (reads per count per millions of reads mapped). To specifically measure the expression of X and Y alleles, we performed SNP calling (gsnap –m 0.05, varscan max missing data 0.4 and minor allele frequency 0.05) and extracted the positions corresponding to XY SNPs located in CDS from the vcf file. For each gene and library, we read the number of reads mapping on reference and variant alleles (corresponding to X and Y alleles respectively) with bedtools intersect [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Quinlan AR, Hall IM. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:841–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3635">78</a>]. Reads numbers were summed by gene and normalized by the total number of reads in the variant file for the library and the length of transcripts.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec42">Organ-specific expression of sex-linked genes in <i>V. vinifera</i>
</h4><p>We retrieved and mapped transcriptomic data of <i>V. sylvestris</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> in several organs and conditions against the genome of <i>V. sylvestris</i> (berries; developing seeds; leaves under normal, drought, and pathogenic conditions; stem; early and mature flower buds; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12). We measured the gene expression levels in the different conditions, and computed an index of organ-specificity (Tau) on normalized expression levels (log (read count per kilobase per millions of mapped reads)). The Tau specificity index ranges between 0 and 1 and typically display two modes near 0 and 1 indicating ubiquitous and organ-specific genes respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Yanai I, Benjamin H, Shmoish M, Chalifa-Caspi V, Shklar M, Ophir R, Bar-Even A, Horn-Saban S, Safran M, Domany E. Genome-wide midrange transcription profiles reveal expression level relationships in human tissue specification. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:650–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3662">79</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec43">Statistical analyses and data visualization</h3><p>Unless stated otherwise, statistical analyses were carried out in R v3.4.4 (2018-03-15) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Team RC: R: a language and environment for statistical computing. 2013." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3674">80</a>]. Data visualization was performed with R or with CIRCOS v0.69-6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, Connors J, Gascoyne R, Horsman D, Jones SJ, Marra MA. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 2009;19:1639–45." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3677">75</a>]. The structural variation among the X, Y, and Yh haplotypes was represented using SimpleSynteny 4.1 (Veltri et al. 2016; <a href="https://www.dveltri.com/simplesynteny/">https://www.dveltri.com/simplesynteny/</a>). Adeneget was used to visualize SNP data in R [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Jombart T. adegenet: a R package for the multivariate analysis of genetic markers. Bioinformatics. 2008;24:1403–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3687">81</a>].</p></div></div></section>
                    <section aria-labelledby="availability-of-data-and-materials" data-title="Availability of data and materials"><div class="c-article-section" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="availability-of-data-and-materials">Availability of data and materials</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-content">
              
              <p>All raw reads, genome assembly, and gene annotation are available in ENA under the bioproject PRJEB37020 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Badouin H, Velt A, Gindraud F, Flutre T, Dumas V, Vautrin S. et al. Accessed 17 August 2020." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3777">82</a>]. The source code of SEX-DETector++ is available at the url <a href="https://gitlab.in2p3.fr/sex-det-family/sex-detector-plusplus">https://gitlab.in2p3.fr/sex-det-family/sex-detector-plusplus</a> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Gindraud F, Guyot D, Käfer J, Picard F, Marais G. Accessed 4 Aug 2020." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d1774e3787">83</a>] available under the GNU General Public License v3.0 and archived at Zenodo [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Gindraud F. Whole-genome PacBio was conducted at the IGM Genomics Center, University of California, San Diego, La Jolla, CA. RNA-seq and cross re-sequencing were performed by the GenomEast platform, a member of the “France Génomique” consortium (ANR-10-INBS-0009). Sylvestris C1-2 Illumina re-sequencing was performed by the Etude du Polymorphisme des Génomes Végétaux (EPGV, INRAE, Evry) facility. We thank Guillaume Barnabé for his help with transcriptome assembly. We thank Jérôme Gouzy for help with curation of the trinity dataset used for gene annotation and Aline Muyle for providing information on the code of SEX-DETector perl, Gisèle Butterlin, and Angélique Ardiller for technical assistance and the UEAV experimental platform (Unité d’Expérimentation Agronomique et Viticole) for plant material maintenance at INRAE Colmar, and Jernej Jakše and Urban Kunej (Univ. Ljubljana) for their help with repeat annotation. The authors would like to acknowledge support of GeT-PlaGe Genomic Platform (INRAE-Toulouse, France, <a href="https://get.genotoul.fr">https://get.genotoul.fr</a>) for providing PacBio BAC sequence data.</p>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Gabriel A. B.+Marais" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Gabriel A. B.+Marais%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>GABM, PH, CR, ED, RT, and RB conceived the research. VD and ED obtained plant material and DNA for cross re-sequencing and genome sequencing. WM obtained DNA for whole-genome PacBio sequencing. TF and RB obtained plant material and DNA for BAC sequencing and with SS defined probes for BAC screening. SV and HBe performed BAC library production and screening, BAC sequencing, and assembly. AV, CR, and JC assembled the genome. RB and TF analyzed BAC sequences and assigned them to sex haplotyes. SS sequenced the gaps between BAC contigs. WM, HBe, ER, KJ, and CR coordinated the sequencing of the genome. ED and CR coordinated the sequencing of the transcriptome. GABM coordinated the sequencing of the cross. JG and RT assembled the transcriptome. AV, CR, and ES annotated the genome and the BAC sequences. FG, FP, DG, JK, and GABM developed SEX-DETector++. HBa, AV, and CR analyzed RNA-seq data. HBa analyzed WGS data and performed structural and molecular evolution analysis of the sex locus. HBa, CR, and RB performed integrative analysis of sex determination. PH, RT, GABM, and RB funded the study. CR, GABM, and RB coordinated the project. HBa and GABM wrote the manuscript, with contributions from all authors. The authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/email/correspondent/c1/new">Hélène Badouin</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/email/correspondent/c2/new">Roberto Bacilieri</a> or <a id="corresp-c3" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/email/correspondent/c3/new">Camille Rustenholz</a> or <a id="corresp-c4" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02131-y/email/correspondent/c4/new">Gabriel A. B. Marais</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
